      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       was the
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_East_Indies" title="Spanish East Indies">
        Philippines
       </a>
       from the beginning of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Philippines" title="Spanish colonization of the Philippines">
        Spanish rule
       </a>
       in the late 16th century, through the conclusion of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">
        Spanish–American War
       </a>
       in 1898. It remained, along with English, as co-official language until 1987. It was at first removed in 1973 by a constitutional change, but after a few months it was re-designated an official language by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidential_decree" title="Presidential decree">
        presidential decree
       </a>
       and remained official until 1987, when the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1987_Constitution" title="1987 Constitution">
        present Constitution
       </a>
       removed its official status, designating it instead as an optional language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-official_languages_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-official_languages-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voluntary_and_optional_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-voluntary_and_optional-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Spanish" title="Philippine Spanish">
        Philippine Spanish
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Español filipino, Castellano filipino
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is a variant of standard
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       by a minority today, though it was quite widespread up to the early 20th century. The variant is very similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Spanish" title="Mexican Spanish">
        Mexican Spanish
       </a>
       , because the Philippines was ruled from
       <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">
        New Spain
       </a>
       in present-day
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       , for over three centuries. During that period, there was much Spanish and Mexican emigration to the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_East_Indies" title="Spanish East Indies">
        Spanish East Indies
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It was the language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
       and the country's first official language, as proclaimed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        Malolos Constitution
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Philippine_Republic" title="First Philippine Republic">
        First Philippine Republic
       </a>
       in 1899. It was the language of commerce, law, politics and the arts during the colonial period and well into the 20th century. It was the main language of many classical writers and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilustrados" title="Ilustrados">
        Ilustrados
       </a>
       such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jose_Rizal" title="Jose Rizal">
        Jose Rizal
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andres_Bonifacio" title="Andres Bonifacio">
        Andres Bonifacio
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Luna" title="Antonio Luna">
        Antonio Luna
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcelo_del_Pilar" title="Marcelo del Pilar">
        Marcelo del Pilar
       </a>
       , to name but a few. It is regulated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Academy_of_the_Spanish_Language" title="Philippine Academy of the Spanish Language">
        Academia Filipina de la Lengua Española
       </a>
       , the main Spanish-language regulating body in the Philippines, and a member of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asociaci%C3%B3n_de_Academias_de_la_Lengua_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Asociación de Academias de la Lengua Española">
        Asociación de Academias de la Lengua Española
       </a>
       , the entity which regulates the Spanish language worldwide.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
        Background
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
        Overview
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Spain_(1785-1873_and_1875-1931).svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Spain_%281785-1873_and_1875-1931%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Spain_(1785-1873_and_1875-1931).svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Flag of Spain (1785–1873 and 1875–1931)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Spanish" title="Philippine Spanish">
        Philippine Spanish
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Spanish" title="Philippine Spanish">
        Spanish
       </a>
       was the language of government, education and trade throughout the three centuries of Spanish rule and continued to serve as a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       until the first half of the 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-florentino_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-florentino-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish was the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Philippine_Republic" title="First Philippine Republic">
        Malolos Republic
       </a>
       , "for the time being", according to the
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        Malolos Constitution
       </a>
       of 1899.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1897and1899_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-1897and1899-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish was also the official language of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantonal_Republic_of_Negros" title="Cantonal Republic of Negros">
        Cantonal Republic of Negros
       </a>
       of 1898 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Zamboanga" title="Republic of Zamboanga">
        Republic of Zamboanga
       </a>
       of 1899.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zamboanga_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-zamboanga-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the early part of the U.S. administration of the Philippine Islands, Spanish was widely spoken and relatively well maintained throughout the American colonial period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-florentino_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-florentino-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even so, Spanish was a language that bound leading men in the Philippines like Trinidad Hermenegildo Pardo de Tavera y Gorricho to
       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Philippines" title="President of the Philippines">
        President
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Osme%C3%B1a" title="Sergio Osmeña">
        Sergio Osmeña
       </a>
       and his successor, President
       <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Roxas" title="Manuel Roxas">
        Manuel Roxas
       </a>
       . As a senator,
       <a href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quezon" title="Manuel L. Quezon">
        Manuel L. Quezon
       </a>
       (later President), delivered a speech in the 1920s entitled "Message to My People" in English and in Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Official_language">
        Official language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Official language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Academy_of_the_Spanish_Language" title="Philippine Academy of the Spanish Language">
        Philippine Academy of the Spanish Language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Spanish remained an official language of government until a new constitution ratified on January 17, 1973 designated English and P
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
        ilipino
       </a>
       , spelled in that draft of the constitution with a "P" instead of the more modern "F", as official languages. Shortly thereafter, Presidential Proclamation No. 155 dated March 15, 1973 ordered that the Spanish language should continue to be recognized as an official language so long as government documents in that language remained untranslated. A later constitution ratified in 1987 designated
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
        Filipino
       </a>
       and English as official languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-official_languages_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-official_languages-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, under this Constitution, Spanish, together with Arabic, was designated an optional and voluntary language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voluntary_and_optional_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-voluntary_and_optional-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">
        Influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are thousands of Spanish
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       in 170 native
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        Philippine languages
       </a>
       , and Spanish orthography has influenced the spelling system used for writing most of these languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Demographics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Filipino" title="Spanish Filipino">
        Spanish Filipino
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to the 1990 Philippine census, there were 2,660 native Spanish speakers in the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue1_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013 there were also 3,325 Spanish residents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, there are 439,000 Spanish speakers with native knowledge,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NativeSpanish_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NativeSpanish-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which accounts for just 0.5% of the population (92,337,852 at the 2010 census).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1998, there were 1.8 million Spanish speakers including those who spoke Spanish as a secondary language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpanishSpeakers_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SpanishSpeakers-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_colonial_period">
        Spanish colonial period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Spanish colonial period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg/220px-Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg/330px-Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg/440px-Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lopez_de_Legazpi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Statue of
         <a href="/wiki/Miguel_L%C3%B3pez_de_Legazpi" title="Miguel López de Legazpi">
          Miguel López de Legazpi
         </a>
         just outside
         <a href="/wiki/Fort_San_Pedro" title="Fort San Pedro">
          Fort San Pedro
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Cebu_City" title="Cebu City">
          Cebu City
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines" title="History of the Philippines">
        History of the Philippines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1521%E2%80%931898)" title="History of the Philippines (1521–1898)">
        History of the Philippines (1521–1898)
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">
        Viceroyalty of New Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Spain" title="History of Spain">
        History of Spain
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Spanish was first introduced to the Philippines in 1565, when the conquistador,
       <a href="/wiki/Miguel_L%C3%B3pez_de_Legazpi" title="Miguel López de Legazpi">
        Miguel López de Legazpi
       </a>
       , founded the first Spanish settlement on the island of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cebu_Island" title="Cebu Island">
        Cebú
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Philippines, ruled first from
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">
        Mexico City
       </a>
       and later from
       <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">
        Madrid
       </a>
       , was a
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish territory
       </a>
       for 333 years (1565–1898).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Schooling was a priority, however. The
       <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">
        Augustinians
       </a>
       opened a school immediately upon arriving in Cebú in 1565; the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan" title="Franciscan">
        Franciscans
       </a>
       followed suit when they arrived in 1577, as did the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">
        Dominicans
       </a>
       when they arrived in 1587. Besides religious instruction, these schools taught how to read and write and imparted industrial and agricultural techniques.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arcilla1994p50_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arcilla1994p50-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Initially, the stance of the Roman Catholic Church and its
       <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">
        missionaries
       </a>
       was to preach to the natives in local languages, not in Spanish. The priests learned the native languages and sometimes employed indigenous peoples as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translator" title="Translator">
        translators
       </a>
       , creating a bilingual class known as Ladinos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Before the 19th century, the natives generally were not taught Spanish. However, there were notable bilingual individuals such as poet-translator
       <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Aquino_de_Bel%C3%A9n" title="Gaspar Aquino de Belén">
        Gaspar Aquino de Belén
       </a>
       . Gaspar produced Christian devotional poetry written in the Roman script in the
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
        Tagalog language
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Pasyon" title="Pasyon">
        Pasyon
       </a>
       is a narrative of the passion, death, and resurrection of Jesus Christ begun by Gaspar Aquino de Belén, which has circulated in many versions. Later, the Spanish-Mexican ballads of chivalry, the
       <a href="/wiki/Corrido" title="Corrido">
        corrido
       </a>
       , provided a model for secular literature. Verse narratives, or komedya, were performed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        regional languages
       </a>
       for the illiterate majority.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the early 17th century, a Tagalog-Chinese printer, Tomás Pinpin, set out to write a book in romanized phonetic script to teach the Tagalogs how to learn
       <a href="/wiki/Names_given_to_the_Spanish_language" title="Names given to the Spanish language">
        Castilian
       </a>
       . His book, published by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_order" title="Dominican order">
        Dominican
       </a>
       press where he worked, appeared in 1610, the same year as Blancas's
       <i>
        Arte
       </i>
       . Unlike the missionary's grammar (which Pinpin had set in type), the Tagalog native's book dealt with the language of the dominant rather than the subordinate other. Pinpin's book was the first such work ever written and published by a Philippine native. As such, it is richly instructive for what it tells us about the interests that animated Tagalog translation and, by implication, Tagalog conversion in the early colonial period.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Illustracion_Filipina_(1892).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="545" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg/220px-La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg/330px-La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg/440px-La_Illustracion_Filipina_%281892%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Illustracion_Filipina_(1892).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Juan_Luna" title="Juan Luna">
          Juan Luna
         </a>
         featured on the cover of a Spanish periodical
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       By law, each town had to build two schools, one for boys and the other for girls, to teach the Spanish language and the
       <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">
        Christian catechism
       </a>
       . There were never enough trained teachers, however, and several provincial schools were mere sheds open to the rain. This discouraged the attendance at school and illiteracy was high in the provinces until the 19th century, when public education was introduced. The conditions were better in larger towns. To qualify as an independent civil town, a
       <a href="/wiki/Barrio" title="Barrio">
        barrio
       </a>
       or group of barrios had to have a priest's residence, a town hall, boys' and girls' schools; streets had to be straight and at right angles to one another so that the town could grow in size; the town had to be near a good water source and land for farming and grazing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Better school conditions in towns and cities led to more effective instruction in the Spanish language and in other subjects. Between 1600 and 1865, a number of colleges and universities were established, which graduated many important colonial officials and church prelates, bishops, and archbishops—several of whom served the churches in
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">
        Hispanic America
       </a>
       . The increased level of education eventually led to the rise of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilustrados" title="Ilustrados">
        Ilustrados
       </a>
       . In 1846, French traveler
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Baptiste_Mallat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Baptiste Mallat (page does not exist)">
        Jean Baptiste Mallat
       </a>
       was surprised at how advanced Philippine schools were.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arcilla1994p50_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arcilla1994p50-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1865, the government inaugurated the Escuela Normal (Normal School), an institute to train future primary school teachers. At the same time, primary schooling was made compulsory for all children. In 1869, a new Spanish constitution brought to the Philippines universal suffrage and a free press.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        El Boletín de Cebú
       </i>
       , the first Spanish newspaper in
       <a href="/wiki/Cebu_City" title="Cebu City">
        Cebu City
       </a>
       , was published in 1886.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Manila, the Spanish language had been more or less widespread, to the point where it has been estimated at around 50% of the population knew Spanish in the late 19th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez-Ponga_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-Ponga-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In his 1898 book "Yesterdays in the Philippines", covering a period beginning in 1893, the American
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Earle_Stevens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joseph Earle Stevens (page does not exist)">
        Joseph Earle Stevens
       </a>
       , an American who resided in Manila from 1893 to 1894, wrote:
      </p>
      <table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
       <tr>
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
         “
        </td>
        <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
         <p>
          Spanish, of course, is the court and commercial language and, except among the uneducated native who have a lingua of their own or among the few members of the
          <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">
           Anglo-Saxon
          </a>
          colony, it has a monopoly everywhere. No one can really get on without it, and even the Chinese come in with their peculiar
          <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">
           pidgin
          </a>
          variety.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
           <a href="#cite_note-23">
            [23]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
         ”
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Long contact between Spanish and the local languages, Chinese dialects, and later Japanese produced a series of pidgins, known as
       <b>
        Bamboo Spanish
       </b>
       , and the Spanish-based creole
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
       . At one point these were the language of a substantial proportion of the Philippine population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unsurprisingly, given that the Philippines was administrated for centuries from
       <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">
        New Spain
       </a>
       in present-day
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       , Philippine Spanish is broadly similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_United_States" title="Spanish language in the United States">
        American Spanish
       </a>
       , not only in vocabulary, but in pronunciation and grammar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the Philippines were not as culturally hispanized as
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">
        Hispanic America
       </a>
       , the Spanish language was the official language used by the civil and judicial administration, and was spoken by the majority of the population and understood by just everyone, especially after the passing of the Education Decree of 1863. By the end of the 19th century, Spanish was a strong second language among the upper classes of Philippine society, having been learned in childhood either directly from parents and grandparents or through tutoring by a local priest.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the time Spanish rule came to an end, Spanish was the language of more than 60% of the population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Schools">
        Schools
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Schools">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippines_education_during_Spanish_rule" title="Philippines education during Spanish rule">
        Philippines education during Spanish rule
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 16th and 17th centuries, the oldest educational institutions in the country were set up by Spanish religious orders. These schools and universities played a crucial role in the development of the Spanish language in the islands. Colegio de Manila in
       <a href="/wiki/Intramuros" title="Intramuros">
        Intramuros
       </a>
       was founded in 1590. The Colegio formally opened in 1595, and was one of the first schools in the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ateneo_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ateneo-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the same year, the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_San_Carlos" title="University of San Carlos">
        University of San Carlos
       </a>
       in Cebú, was established as the Colegio de San Ildefonso by the Jesuits. In 1611, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Santo_Tom%C3%A1s" title="University of Santo Tomás">
        University of Santo Tomás
       </a>
       , considered as the oldest existing university in Asia, was inaugurated in Manila by the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">
        Dominicans
       </a>
       . In the 18th century, fluent male Spanish speakers in the Philippines were generally the graduates of these schools, as well as of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colegio_de_San_Juan_de_Letr%C3%A1n" title="Colegio de San Juan de Letrán">
        Colegio de San Juan de Letrán
       </a>
       , established in 1620. In 1706, a convent school for Philippine women known as Beaterios was established. It admitted both Spanish and native girls, and taught Religion, Reading, Writing and Arithmetic with Music and Embroidery. Female graduates from Beaterios were fluent in the language as well. In 1859,
       <a href="/wiki/Ateneo_de_Manila_University" title="Ateneo de Manila University">
        Ateneo de Manila University
       </a>
       was established by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">
        Jesuits
       </a>
       as the Escuela Municipal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ateneo_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ateneo-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1863, Queen
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isabel_II_of_Spain" title="Isabel II of Spain">
        Isabel II of Spain
       </a>
       decreed the establishment of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" title="Public school (government funded)">
        public school
       </a>
       system, following the requests of the Spanish authorities in the islands, who saw the need of teaching Spanish to the wider population. The primary instruction and the teaching of the Spanish language was compulsory. The Educational Decree provided for the establishment of at least one primary school for boys and girls in each town and governed by the municipal government. Normal school for male teachers was established and was supervised by the Jesuits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1866, the total population of the Philippines was only 4,411,261. The total public schools was 841 for boys and 833 for girls and the total number of children attending these schools was 135,098 boys and 95,260 girls. In 1892, the number of schools had increased to 2,137, 1,087 of which were for boys and 1,050 for girls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This measure was at the vanguard of contemporary Asian countries, and led to an important class of educated natives which sometimes followed their studies abroad, like national hero
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Rizal" title="José Rizal">
        José Rizal
       </a>
       , who studied in Europe. This class of writers, poets and intellectuals is often referred to as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilustrados" title="Ilustrados">
        Ilustrados
       </a>
       . Ironically, it was during the initial years of American occupation in the early 20th century, that Spanish literature and press flourished. This was the result both of a majority of Spanish-speaking population, as well as the partial freedom of the press which the American rulers allowed.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Filipino_nationalism_and_19th_century_revolutionary_governments">
        Filipino nationalism and 19th century revolutionary governments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Filipino nationalism and 19th century revolutionary governments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bandera_03.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="510" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bandera_03.jpg/220px-Bandera_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bandera_03.jpg/330px-Bandera_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bandera_03.jpg/440px-Bandera_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bandera_03.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Early flag of the
         <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolutionary_Army" title="Philippine Revolutionary Army">
          Filipino revolutionaries
         </a>
         ("Long live the Philippine Republic!"). The first two constitutions were written in Spanish.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_nationalism" title="Filipino nationalism">
        Filipino nationalism
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_revolts_against_Spain" title="Philippine revolts against Spain">
        Philippine revolts against Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_Movement" title="Propaganda Movement">
        Propaganda Movement
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Solidaridad" title="La Solidaridad">
        La Solidaridad
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Before the 19th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_revolts_against_Spain" title="Philippine revolts against Spain">
        Philippine revolts
       </a>
       were small-scale and did not extend beyond linguistic boundaries. Thus, they were easily neutralized by Spanish forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstfilipino_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-firstfilipino-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With the small period of the spread of Spanish through a free public school system (1863) and the rise of an educated class, nationalists from different parts of the archipelago were able to communicate in a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        common language
       </a>
       . José Rizal's novels,
       <a href="/wiki/Graciano_L%C3%B3pez_Jaena" title="Graciano López Jaena">
        Graciano López Jaena
       </a>
       's satirical articles,
       <a href="/wiki/Marcelo_H._del_Pilar" title="Marcelo H. del Pilar">
        Marcelo H. del Pilar
       </a>
       's anti-clerical manifestos, the bi-weekly
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Solidaridad" title="La Solidaridad">
         La Solidaridad
        </a>
       </i>
       which was published in Spain, and other materials in awakening nationalism were written in Spanish. The
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
       fought for reforms and later for independence from Spain. However, it did not oppose Spain's
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_influence_on_Filipino_culture" title="Spanish influence on Filipino culture">
        cultural legacy
       </a>
       in the islands or the Spanish language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even Graciano López Jaena's La Solidaridad article in 1889 praised the young women of
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos" title="Malolos">
        Malolos
       </a>
       who petitioned to
       <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Philippines#Spanish_Colonial_Government_(1764–1898)" title="Governor-General of the Philippines">
        Governor-General
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valeriano_Weyler,_1st_Duke_of_Rub%C3%AD" title="Valeriano Weyler, 1st Duke of Rubí">
        Valeriano Weyler
       </a>
       to open a night school to teach the Spanish language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, the
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Congress" title="Malolos Congress">
        Malolos Congress
       </a>
       of 1899 chose Spanish as the official language. According to
       <a href="/wiki/Horacio_de_la_Costa" title="Horacio de la Costa">
        Horacio de la Costa
       </a>
       , nationalism would not have been possible without Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstfilipino_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-firstfilipino-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       by then increasingly aware of nationalistic ideas and independence movements in other countries.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La-solidaridad2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="264" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/La-solidaridad2.jpg/220px-La-solidaridad2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/La-solidaridad2.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La-solidaridad2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
          Propaganda
         </a>
         in Spanish
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Spanish was used by the first Filipino patriots like
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Rizal" title="José Rizal">
        José Rizal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Bonifacio" title="Andrés Bonifacio">
        Andrés Bonifacio
       </a>
       and, to a lesser extent,
       <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Aguinaldo" title="Emilio Aguinaldo">
        Emilio Aguinaldo
       </a>
       . The 1896 Biak-na-Bato Constitution and the 1898 Malolos Constitution were both written in Spanish. Neither specified a national language, but both recognised the continuing use of Spanish in Philippine life and legislation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1897and1899_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1897and1899-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andrewgonzalez_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-andrewgonzalez-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Aguinaldo was more comfortable speaking Tagalog.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish was used to write the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Biak-na-Bato" title="Constitution of Biak-na-Bato">
        Constitution of Biak-na-Bato
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        Malolos Constitution
       </a>
       , the original
       <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">
        national anthem
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Himno Nacional Filipino
       </i>
       , as well as nationalistic propaganda material and literature.
      </p>
      <p>
       The country's first two constitutions and historic novels were written in Spanish. While widely understood by the majority of the population, Spanish at this time was the unifying language since Tagalog was not as prominent or ubiquitous as it is today and each region had their own culture and language, and would rather speak in their local languages. Before the spread of Filipino nationalism, the natives of each region still thought of themselves as
       <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_people" title="Ilocano people">
        Ilocano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_people" title="Cebuano people">
        Cebuano
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicolano_people" title="Bicolano people">
        Bicolano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Waray_people" title="Waray people">
        Waray
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">
        Tagalog
       </a>
       etc., and not as Filipinos.
      </p>
      <p>
       The term "Filipino" originally referred to the natives of the Philippines themselves. It was Pedro Chirino, a Spanish Jesuit, who first called the natives "Filipinos," in his book
       <i>
        Relación de las Islas Filipinas
       </i>
       (Rome, 1604). However, during their 333-year rule of the Philippines, the Spanish rulers preferred to call the natives
       <i>
        Indios
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Also during the colonial era, the Spaniards born in the Philippines, who were more known as insulares, criollos, or Creoles, were also called "Filipinos."
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spanish-born
       </a>
       Spaniards or mainland Spaniards residing in the Philippines were referred to as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peninsulares" title="Peninsulares">
        Peninsulares
       </a>
       . Peoples born in Spanish America or in the North American continent of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">
        New Spain
       </a>
       who were residing in the Philippines were collectively referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_mestizo#Colonial_caste_system" title="Filipino mestizo">
        Americanos
       </a>
       . The Catholic
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">
        Austronesian peoples
       </a>
       of the Philippines were referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_mestizo#Colonial_caste_system" title="Filipino mestizo">
        Indios
       </a>
       and for those who were practicing the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_faith" title="Islamic faith">
        Islamic faith
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Moro_people" title="Moro people">
        Moros
       </a>
       . The indigenous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeta_peoples" title="Aeta peoples">
        Aetas
       </a>
       were referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_mestizo#Colonial_caste_system" title="Filipino mestizo">
        Negritos
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Filipino" title="Chinese Filipino">
        Chinese
       </a>
       settlers were called
       <a href="/wiki/Sangley" title="Sangley">
        Sangleyes
       </a>
       . Japanese settlers were called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_settlement_in_the_Philippines" title="Japanese settlement in the Philippines">
        Japoneses
       </a>
       . Those of mixed ancestry were referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_mestizo#Colonial_caste_system" title="Filipino mestizo">
        Mestizos
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tornatr%C3%A1s" title="Tornatrás">
        Tornatrás
       </a>
       . In the 1800s, the term "Filipino" gradually became synonymous to anyone born in the Philippines regardless of ethnicity through the effort of the
       <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people" title="Criollo people">
        Insulares
       </a>
       , from whom,
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_nationalism" title="Filipino nationalism">
        Filipino nationalism
       </a>
       began.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1863, the Spanish language was taught freely when a primary public school system was set up for the entire population. The Spanish-speaking
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilustrados" title="Ilustrados">
        Ilustrados
       </a>
       (The Enlightened Ones), which included the Insulares, the Indios, the Mestizos, the Tornatrás, etc., were the educated elite who promoted and propagated nationalism and a modern Filipino consciousness. The Ilustrados and later writers formed the basis of
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_literature_in_Spanish" title="Philippine literature in Spanish">
        Philippine Classical Literature
       </a>
       which developed in the 19th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       José Rizal propagated Filipino consciousness and identity in Spanish. One material highly instrumental in developing nationalism was the novels entitled
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noli_Me_Tangere_(novel)" title="Noli Me Tangere (novel)">
         Noli Me Tangere
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Filibusterismo" title="El Filibusterismo">
         El Filibusterismo
        </a>
       </i>
       which exposed the abuses of the colonial government and clergy composed of Peninsulares. The novels' very own notoriety propelled its popularity even more among Filipinos. Reading it was forbidden because it exposed and parodied the Peninsulares in the Philippine Islands.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Philippine.E2.80.93American_War">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Philippine–American_War">
        Philippine–American War
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Philippine–American War">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">
        Philippine–American War
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The revolutionary
       <a href="/wiki/First_Philippine_Republic" title="First Philippine Republic">
        Malolos Republic
       </a>
       of 1899 designated the Spanish language for official use in its
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        constitution
       </a>
       , drawn up during the Constitutional Convention in
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos" title="Malolos">
        Malolos
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bulacan" title="Bulacan">
        Bulacan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1897and1899_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-1897and1899-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During this period, the nascent republic published a number of laws, acts, decrees, and other official issuances. These were published variously in the Spanish, English, and Tagalog languages, with the Spanish language predominating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish was also designated the official language of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantonal_Republic_of_Negros" title="Cantonal Republic of Negros">
        Cantonal Republic of Negros
       </a>
       of 1898 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Zamboanga" title="Republic of Zamboanga">
        Republic of Zamboanga
       </a>
       of 1899.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zamboanga_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-zamboanga-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many Spanish-speaking Filipino families perished during the Philippine–American War. According to the historian James B. Goodno, author of the
       <i>
        Philippines: Land of Broken Promises
       </i>
       (New York, 1998), one-sixth of the total population of Filipinos or about 1.5 million died as a direct result of the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="American_colonial_period">
        American colonial period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: American colonial period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1898%E2%80%931946)" title="History of the Philippines (1898–1946)">
        History of the Philippines (1898–1946)
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">
        American imperialism
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States" title="History of the United States">
        History of the United States
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="822" data-file-width="596" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg/220px-Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg/330px-Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg/440px-Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jones_Law_poster_Philippines_1916.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A poster advertising the
         <a href="/wiki/Jones_Law_(Philippines)" title="Jones Law (Philippines)">
          Jones Law
         </a>
         of 1916 in Spanish,
         <i>
          The Glorious Jones Law
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       With the era of the Philippines as a Spanish colony with its people as Spanish citizens
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       having just ended, a considerable amount of media, newspapers, radios, and government proceedings were still written and produced in Spanish. By law, the
       <a href="/wiki/Taft_Commission" title="Taft Commission">
        Taft Commission
       </a>
       allowed their guests to use the language of their choice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-escalante2007p88_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-escalante2007p88-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ironically, the partial freedom of the press allowed by the American rulers served to further promote Spanish-language literacy among the masses. Even in the early 20th century, a
       <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">
        hegemony
       </a>
       of Spanish language was still in force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-florentino_3-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-florentino-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While the census of 1903 and of 1905 officially reported that the number of Spanish-speakers have never exceeded 10% of the total population during the final decade of the 19th century, it only considered Spanish speakers as their first and only language. It disregarded the Catholic
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Filipino" title="Chinese Filipino">
        Chinese Filipinos
       </a>
       , many of whom spoke Spanish, and the creole-speaking communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, those who were academically instructed in the public school system also used Spanish as their second or third language. These together would have placed the numbers at more than 60% of the 9,000,000 Filipinos of that era as Spanish-speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Eighth Annual Report by the Director of Education, David P. Barrows, dated August 1, 1908, the following observations were made about the use and extension of the Spanish language in the Philippines:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistics_6-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-statistics-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Of the adult population, including persons of mature years and social influence, the number speaking English is relatively small. This class speaks Spanish, and as it is the most prominent and important class of people in the Islands, Spanish continues to be the most important language spoken in political, journalistic and commercial circles.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       A 1916 report by
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jones_Ford" title="Henry Jones Ford">
        Henry Jones Ford
       </a>
       to President
       <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">
        Woodrow Wilson
       </a>
       said
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        ...as I traveled through the Philippine Islands, using ordinary transportation and mixing with all classes of people under all conditions. Although based on the school statistics it is said that more Filipinos speak English than any other language, no one can be in agreement with this declaration if they base their assessment on what they hear...
       </p>
       <p>
        Spanish is everywhere the language of business and social intercourse...In order for anyone to obtain prompt service from anyone, Spanish turns out to be more useful than English...And outside of Manila it is almost indispensable. The Americans who travel around all the islands customarily use it.
       </p>
       The use of Spanish as an official language has been extended to January 1, 1920. Its general use seems to be spreading. Natives acquiring it learn it as a living speech. Everywhere they hear it spoken by leading people of the community and their ears are trained to its pronunciation. On the other hand, they (the natives) are practically without phonic standards in acquiring English and the result is that they learn it as a book language rather than as a living speech.
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Henry Jones Ford
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Although the English language had begun to be heavily promoted and used as the medium of education and government proceedings, the majority of literature produced by indigenous Filipinos during this period was in Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez-Ponga_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-Ponga-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Among the great Filipino literary writers of the period were Fernando M.
       <sup>
        <u>
         a
        </u>
       </sup>
       Guerrero, Rafael Palma, Cecilio Apóstol,
       <a href="/wiki/Jes%C3%BAs_Balmori" title="Jesús Balmori">
        Jesús Balmori
       </a>
       , Manuel Bernabé, Trinidad Pardo de Tavera and Teodoro M. Kalaw.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This explosion of Spanish language in
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_literature_in_Spanish" title="Philippine literature in Spanish">
        Philippine literature
       </a>
       occurred because the middle and upper class Filipinos were educated in Spanish and Spanish language as a subject was offered in public schools. In 1936, Philippine
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">
        sound films
       </a>
       in Spanish began to be produced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Filipinos experienced a partial freedom of expression, since the American authorities weren't too receptive to Filipino writers and intellectuals during most of the colonial period. As a result, Spanish had become the most important language in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-florentino_3-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-florentino-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Until the Second World War, Spanish was the language of Manila.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez-Ponga_22-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-Ponga-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the war, the English-speaking U.S. having won three wars [in 1898, against Spain (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">
        Spanish–American War
       </a>
       ); in 1913 (from
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">
        Philippine–American War
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Moro_Rebellion" title="Moro Rebellion">
        Moro Rebellion
       </a>
       ) against the Filipino independence; in 1945 against Japan (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippines_Campaign_(1944%E2%80%9345)" title="Philippines Campaign (1944–45)">
        Philippines Campaign
       </a>
       )], the English language was imposed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez-Ponga_22-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-Ponga-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_the_Spanish_language">
        Decline of the Spanish language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Decline of the Spanish language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">
        Language shift
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">
        Language death
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="1600" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg/220px-Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg/330px-Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg/440px-Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manila_Walled_City_Destruction_May_1945.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The destruction of
         <a href="/wiki/Intramuros" title="Intramuros">
          Intramuros
         </a>
         in May 1945 after the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manila_(1945)" title="Battle of Manila (1945)">
          Battle of Manila
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Spanish language flourished in the first two decades of the 20th century due to the partial freedom of the press and as an act of defiance against the new rulers. Spanish declined due to the imposition of English as the official language and medium of instruction in schools and universities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forgotten_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-forgotten-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-libradaavelino_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-libradaavelino-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The American administration increasingly forced editorials and newspapers to switch to English, leaving Spanish in a marginal position, so that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zobel_de_Ayala_family" title="Zobel de Ayala family">
        Enrique Zóbel de Ayala
       </a>
       founded the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Academy_of_the_Spanish_Language" title="Philippine Academy of the Spanish Language">
        Academia Filipina de la Lengua Española
       </a>
       and the Premio Zóbel in 1924 to help maintain and develop the use of Spanish among the Filipino people.
      </p>
      <p>
       It did not help when some Filipino nationalists and nationalist
       <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
        historiographers
       </a>
       during the American Colonial Period who took their liberal ideas from the writings of the 19th century
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_Movement" title="Propaganda Movement">
        Filipino Propaganda
       </a>
       which portrayed Spain and all things Spanish as negative or evil. Therefore, Spanish as a language was
       <a href="/wiki/Demonization" title="Demonization">
        demonized
       </a>
       as a sad reminder of the past.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These ideas gradually inculcated into the minds of the young generation of Filipinos (during and after the American administration) who used those history textbooks at school that tended to generalize all Spaniards as villains due to lack of emphasis on
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_people_of_Spanish_ancestry" title="Filipino people of Spanish ancestry">
        Filipino people of Spanish ancestry
       </a>
       who were also against the local Spanish government and clergy and also fought and died for the sake of freedom during the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_revolts_against_Spain#19th_century" title="Philippine revolts against Spain">
        19th century revolts
       </a>
       , during the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
       , during the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">
        Philippine–American War
       </a>
       and during World War II.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the 1940s as children educated in English became adults, the Spanish language was starting to decline rapidly. Still, a very significant community of Filipino Spanish-speakers lived in the bigger cities, with a total population of roughly 300,000. However, with the destruction of
       <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">
        Manila
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_the_Philippines" title="Japanese occupation of the Philippines">
        Japanese occupation
       </a>
       in World War II, the heart of the Spanish language in the Philippines was dismantled.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many Spanish-speaking Filipino families perished during the massacre and bombing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cities_of_the_Philippines" title="Cities of the Philippines">
        cities
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_the_Philippines" title="Municipalities of the Philippines">
        municipalities
       </a>
       between 1942 and 1945. By the end of the war, an estimated 1 million Filipinos lost their lives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of those Spanish-speakers who survived were forced to migrate in the later years.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the war, Spanish became increasingly marginalized at an official level. As English and American-influenced
       <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">
        pop culture
       </a>
       increased, the use of Spanish in all aspects gradually declined. In 1962, when President
       <a href="/wiki/Diosdado_Macapagal" title="Diosdado Macapagal">
        Diosdado Macapagal
       </a>
       decreed that the Philippines mark
       <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Philippines)" title="Independence Day (Philippines)">
        independence day
       </a>
       on June 12 instead of July 4 which the country gained complete
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_Day_(Philippines)" title="Republic Day (Philippines)">
        independence from the United States
       </a>
       , it revealed a tendency to paint Spain as the villain and the United States as saviour, or the more benevolent colonial power.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Spanish language and Hispanic culture was demonized again.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forgotten_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-forgotten-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
          <span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2014)">
           not in citation given
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In 1973, Spanish briefly lost its status as an official language of the Philippines, was quickly redesignated as an official language, and finally did lose official status with the ratification of a subsequent constitution in 1987.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-official_languages_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-official_languages-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="21st-century_developments">
        21st-century developments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 21st-century developments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">
        Language revitalization
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The 21st century has seen a revival of interest in the language, with the numbers of those studying it formally at college or taking private courses rising markedly in recent years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newprospects_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-newprospects-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, the
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="Constitution of the Philippines">
        Philippine constitution
       </a>
       provides that Spanish shall be promoted on a voluntary and optional basis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A great portion of the history of the Philippines is written in Spanish and, up until recently, many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_title" title="Land title">
        land titles
       </a>
       , contracts, newspapers and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literature_of_the_Philippines" title="Literature of the Philippines">
        literature
       </a>
       were still written in Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, Spanish is being somewhat revived in the Philippines by groups rallying to make it a compulsory subject in school.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Republic Act No. 9187 was approved on February 5, 2003 and signed by President
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gloria_Macapagal-Arroyo" title="Gloria Macapagal-Arroyo">
        Gloria Macapagal-Arroyo
       </a>
       declaring June 30 of every year as
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93Spanish_Friendship_Day" title="Philippine–Spanish Friendship Day">
        Philippine–Spanish Friendship Day
       </a>
       to commemorate the cultural and historical ties, friendship and cooperation between the Philippines and Spain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On July 3, 2006, the
       <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Local_Authorities_of_the_Philippines" title="Union of Local Authorities of the Philippines">
        Union of Local Authorities of the Philippines
       </a>
       created Resolution No. 2006-028 urging the national government to support and promote the teaching of the Spanish language in all public and private universities and colleges in the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On December 17, 2007, the
       <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Education_(Philippines)" title="Department of Education (Philippines)">
        Department of Education
       </a>
       issued Memorandum No. 490, s. 2007 encouraging secondary schools to offer basic and advance Spanish in the 3rd and 4th year levels respectively, as an elective.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2008
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , there was a growing demand for Spanish-speaking agents in the call center industry as well as in the business process outsourcing in the Philippines for the Spanish and American market. Around 7,000 students were enrolled in the Spanish language classes of the Instituto Cervantes de Manila for the school year 2007–2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On December 11, 2008, the Department of Education issued Memorandum No. 560, s. 2008 that shall implement the Special Program in Foreign Language on a pilot basis starting school year 2009–2010. The program shall initially offer Spanish as a foreign language in one school per region, at two classes of 35 students each, per school.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2009, the Spanish government has offered to fund a project and even offered scholarship grants to Spain for public school teachers and students who would like to study Spanish or take up a master's degree in four top universities in Spain. The Spanish government has been funding the ongoing pilot teacher training program about the Spanish language, involving two months of face-to-face classes and a 10-month on-line component.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Clásicos Hispanofilipinos
       </i>
       is a project of Instituto Cervantes de Manila which aims to promote Filipino heritage and preserve and reintroduce the works of great Fil-Hispanic authors of the early 20th century to the new generation of Filipino
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanophone" title="Hispanophone">
        Hispanophones
       </a>
       . The Spanish novel of
       <a href="/wiki/Jes%C3%BAs_Balmori" title="Jesús Balmori">
        Jesús Balmori
       </a>
       entitled
       <i>
        Los Pájaros de Fuego
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Birds of Fire
       </i>
